srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/River_Sindh.jpg/420px-River_Sindh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/River_Sindh.jpg/560px-River_Sindh.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:River_Sindh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indus River in <a href="/wiki/Kharmang_District" title="Kharmang District">Kharmang District</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <b>Indus River</b> (locally called <b>Mehran</b> or <b>Sindhu</b>) is one of the longest rivers in Asia. Originating in the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a> in the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Lake_Manasarovar" title="Lake Manasarovar">Lake Manasarovar</a>, the river runs a course through the <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a> region of Indian state <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir" title="Jammu and Kashmir">Jammu and Kashmir</a>, towards the <a href="/wiki/Gilgit-Baltistan" title="Gilgit-Baltistan">Gilgit-Baltistan</a> region of Pakistan and the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindukush</a> ranges, and then flows in a southerly direction along the entire length of Pakistan to merge into the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Sea" title="Arabian Sea">Arabian Sea</a> near the port city of <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> It is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_rivers_in_Pakistan" title="List of rivers in Pakistan">longest river</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/National_river" title="National river">national river</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The river has a total <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drainage_area" title="Drainage area">drainage area</a> exceeding 1,165,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (450,000&#160;sq&#160;mi). Its estimated annual flow stands at around 243&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (58&#160;cu&#160;mi), twice that of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nile_River" title="Nile River">Nile River</a> and three times that of the <a href="/wiki/Tigris" title="Tigris">Tigris</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euphrates" title="Euphrates">Euphrates</a> rivers combined, making it the twenty-first largest river in the world in terms of annual flow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Zanskar_River" title="Zanskar River">Zanskar</a> is its left bank tributary in <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>. In the plains, its left bank tributary is the <a href="/wiki/Panjnad_River" title="Panjnad River">Panjnad</a> which itself has five major tributaries, namely, the <a href="/wiki/Chenab_River" title="Chenab River">Chenab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Jhelum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Beas</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sutlej" title="Sutlej">Sutlej</a>. Its principal right bank tributaries are the <a href="/wiki/Shyok_River" title="Shyok River">Shyok</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gilgit_River" title="Gilgit River">Gilgit</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kabul_River" title="Kabul River">Kabul</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Gomal_River" title="Gomal River">Gomal</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kurram_River" title="Kurram River">Kurram</a>. Beginning in a mountain spring and fed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaciers" title="Glaciers">glaciers</a> and rivers in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>, the river supports <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ecosystems" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a> of temperate forests, <a href="/wiki/Plain" title="Plain">plains</a> and arid countryside.\n</p><p>The northern part of the Indus Valley, with its tributaries, forms the <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a> region, while the lower course of the Indus is known as <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> and ends in a large <a href="/wiki/Indus_River_Delta" title="Indus River Delta">delta</a>. The river has historically been important to many cultures of the region. The 3rd millennium BC saw the rise of <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">a major urban civilization</a> of the Bronze Age. During the 2nd millennium BC, the Punjab region was mentioned in the hymns of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a> as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sapta_Sindhu" title="Sapta Sindhu">Sapta Sindhu</a></i> and the <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a> as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hapta_Hindu" title="Hapta Hindu">Hapta Hindu</a></i> (both terms meaning "seven rivers"). Early historical kingdoms that arose in the Indus Valley include <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandh&#257;ra</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Ror_dynasty" title="Ror dynasty">Ror dynasty</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sauvira" title="Sauvira">Sauv&#299;ra</a>. The Indus River came into the knowledge of the West early in the Classical Period, when King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persia" title="Persia">Persia</a> sent his Greek subject <a href="/wiki/Scylax_of_Caryanda" title="Scylax of Caryanda">Scylax of Caryanda</a> to explore the river, ca. 515&#160;BC.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_names">Etymology and names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology and names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>This river was known to the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_peoples" title="Indo-Aryan peoples">ancient Indians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> as <i>Sindhu</i> and the Persians as <i>Hindu</i> which was regarded by both of them as "the border river".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The variation between the two names is explained by the Old Iranian sound change <i>*s</i> &gt; <i>h</i>, which occurred between 850&#8211;600 BCE according to <a href="/wiki/Asko_Parpola" title="Asko Parpola">Asko Parpola</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEParpola2015Chapter_9_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEParpola2015Chapter_9-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prasad2017_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prasad2017-11">[11]</a></sup> From the Persian <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>, the name passed to the <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greeks</a> as <i>Ind&#243;s</i> (&#7992;&#957;&#948;&#972;&#962;).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> It was adopted by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a> as <i>Indus</i>.\n</p><p>The meaning of <i>Sindhu</i> as a "large body of water, sea, or ocean" is a later meaning in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_Sanskrit" title="Classical Sanskrit">Classical Sanskrit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mountjoy2004_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mountjoy2004-13">[13]</a></sup> A later Persian name for the river was <i>Darya</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> which similarly has the connotations of large body of water and sea.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Other variants of the name <i>Sindhu</i> include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Assyrians" title="Ancient Assyrians">Assyrian</a> <i>Sinda</i> (as early as the 7th century BC), Persian  <i>Ab-e-sind</i>,  Pashto  <i>Abasind</i>, Arab <i>Al-Sind</i>,  Chinese <i>Sintow</i>, and Javanese  <i><a href="/wiki/Santri" title="Santri">Santri</a></i>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indus_and_the_name_of_India">Indus and the name of India</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Indus and the name of India">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p><i>India</i> is a Greek and Latin term for "the country of the River Indus". The region through which the river drains into sea is called <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> and owes its name to the river (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>: <i>Sindhu</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Megasthenes" title="Megasthenes">Megasthenes\'</a> book <i>Indica</i> derives its name from the river\'s Greek name, "Ind&#243;s" (<i>&#7992;&#957;&#948;&#972;&#962;</i>), and describes <a href="/wiki/Nearchus" title="Nearchus">Nearchus\'s</a> contemporaneous account of how <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> crossed the river. The <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greeks</a> referred to the Indians (people of present-day northwest <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>) as "Ind&#243;i" (<i>&#7992;&#957;&#948;&#959;&#943;</i>), literally meaning "the people of the Indus".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKuiper201086_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKuiper201086-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rigveda_and_the_Indus">Rigveda and the Indus</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Rigveda and the Indus">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Rigveda" title="Rigveda">Rigveda</a> describes <a href="/wiki/Rigvedic_rivers" title="Rigvedic rivers">several rivers</a>, including one named "Sindhu". The Rigvedic "Sindhu" is thought to be the present-day Indus river. It is attested 176 times in its text, 94 times in the plural, and most often used in the generic sense of "river". In the Rigveda, notably in the later hymns, the meaning of the word is narrowed to refer to the Indus river in particular, e.g. in the list of rivers mentioned in the hymn of <i><a href="/wiki/Nadistuti_sukta" title="Nadistuti sukta">Nadistuti sukta</a></i>. The Rigvedic hymns apply a feminine gender to all the rivers mentioned therein, except the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bramhaputra" title="Bramhaputra">Bramhaputra</a> and the "Sindhu" which carry the masculine gender. This gender usage could mean that the Sindhu river was believed to be a warrior, and thus one of the greatest among all the rivers in the whole world.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_names">Other names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Other names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>In other languages of the region, the river is known as &#2360;&#2367;&#2344;&#2381;&#2343;&#2369; <i>(Sindhu)</i> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindi_language" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nepali_language" title="Nepali language">Nepali</a>, &#1587;&#1606;&#1676;&#1608; (<i>Sindhu</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi</a>, <span class="Nastaliq" dir="rtl" style="font-family: \'Jameel Noori Nastaleeq\', \'Urdu Typesetting\', \'Noto Nastaliq Urdu\', \'Noto Nastaliq Urdu Draft\', \'Hussaini Nastaleeq\', \'AlQalam Taj Nastaleeq\', IranNastaliq, \'Awami Nastaliq\', \'Awami Nastaliq Beta3\', \'Awami Nastaliq Beta2\', \'Awami Nastaliq Beta1\', \'Nafees Nastaleeq\', \'Nafees Nastaleeq v1.01\', \'Pak Nastaleeq\', \'PDMS_Jauhar\', \'Alvi Lahori Nastaleeq\'; font-size: 110%; font-style: normal;" title="Nastaliq">&#1587;&#1606;&#1583;&#1726;</span>&#8236; (<i>Sindh</i>) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shahmukhi" title="Shahmukhi">Shahmukhi Punjabi</a>, &#2616;&#2623;&#2672;&#2599; &#2600;&#2598;&#2624; (<i>Sindh Nad&#299;</i>) in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurmukh%C4%AB" title="Gurmukh&#299;">Gurmukh&#299; Punjabi</a>, &#1575;&#1576;&#1575;&#1587;&#1610;&#1606; (<i>Ab&#257;sin</i> lit. "Father of Rivers") in <a href="/wiki/Pashto" title="Pashto">Pashto</a>, &#1606;&#1607;&#1585; &#1575;&#1604;&#1587;&#1606;&#1583; (<i>Nahar al-Sind</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">Arabic</a>, &#3942;&#3962;&#3908;&#3851;&#3906;&#3962;&#3851;&#3906;&#3929;&#3908;&#3851;&#3924;&#3964;&#3853; (<a href="/wiki/S%C3%AAngg%C3%AA_Zangbo" title="S&#234;ngg&#234; Zangbo"><i>singi khamban</i></a> lit. "Lion River" or <i>Lion Spring</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Standard_Tibetan" title="Standard Tibetan">Tibetan</a>, <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text">&#21360;&#24230;</span> (<i>Y&#236;nd&#249;</i>) in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>, and <i>Nilab</i> in <a href="/wiki/Turki" title="Turki">Turki</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Description">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Babur_crossing_the_Indus_in_the_heat_of_battle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="398" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Babur_crossing_the_Indus_in_the_heat_of_battle.jpg/220px-Babur_crossing_the_Indus_in_the_heat_of_battle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Babur_crossing_the_Indus_in_the_heat_of_battle.jpg/330px-Babur_crossing_the_Indus_in_the_heat_of_battle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Babur_crossing_the_Indus_in_the_heat_of_battle.jpg/440px-Babur_crossing_the_Indus_in_the_heat_of_battle.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Babur_crossing_the_Indus_in_the_heat_of_battle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> crossing the Indus River.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Indus River provides key water resources for <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Pakistan" title="Economy of Pakistan">Pakistan\'s economy</a> &#8211; especially the <i>breadbasket</i> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_(Pakistan)" title="Punjab (Pakistan)">Punjab province</a>, which accounts for most of the nation\'s agricultural production, and Sindh. The word Punjab means "land of five rivers" and the five rivers are <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Jhelum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chenab_River" title="Chenab River">Chenab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Beas</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sutlej_River" title="Sutlej River">Sutlej</a>, all of which finally flow into the Indus. The Indus also supports many heavy industries and provides the main supply of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potable_water" title="Potable water">potable water</a> in Pakistan.\n</p><p>The ultimate source of the Indus is in <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a>; the river begins at the confluence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sengge_Zangbo" title="Sengge Zangbo">Sengge Zangbo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gar_Tsangpo" title="Gar Tsangpo">Gar Tsangpo</a> rivers that drain the Nganglong Kangri and Gangdise Shan (Gang Rinpoche, Mt. Kailash) mountain ranges. The Indus then flows northwest through <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>, <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Baltistan" title="Baltistan">Baltistan</a> into <a href="/wiki/Gilgit" title="Gilgit">Gilgit</a>, just south of the <a href="/wiki/Karakoram" title="Karakoram">Karakoram</a> range. The <a href="/wiki/Shyok_River" title="Shyok River">Shyok</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shigar" title="Shigar">Shigar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gilgit_River" title="Gilgit River">Gilgit</a> rivers carry glacial waters into the main river. It gradually bends to the south and descends into the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Punjab_plains&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Punjab plains (page does not exist)">Punjab plains</a> at <a href="/wiki/Kalabagh" title="Kalabagh">Kalabagh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a>. The Indus passes gigantic gorges 4,500&#8211;5,200 metres (15,000&#8211;17,000 feet) deep near the <a href="/wiki/Nanga_Parbat" title="Nanga Parbat">Nanga Parbat</a> <a href="/wiki/Massif" title="Massif">massif</a>. It flows swiftly across <a href="/wiki/Hazara,_Pakistan" title="Hazara, Pakistan">Hazara</a> and is dammed at the <a href="/wiki/Tarbela_Dam" title="Tarbela Dam">Tarbela Reservoir</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Kabul_River" title="Kabul River">Kabul River</a> joins it near <a href="/wiki/Attock" title="Attock">Attock</a>. The remainder of its route to the sea is in the plains of the Punjab<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> and Sindh, where the flow of the river becomes slow and highly braided. It is joined by the <a href="/wiki/Panjnad_River" title="Panjnad River">Panjnad</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mithankot" title="Mithankot">Mithankot</a>. Beyond this confluence, the river, at one time, was named the <i>Satnad River</i> (<i>sat</i> = "seven", <i>nad&#299;</i> = "river"), as the river now carried the waters of the <a href="/wiki/Kabul_River" title="Kabul River">Kabul River</a>, the Indus River and the five Punjab rivers. Passing by <a href="/wiki/Jamshoro" title="Jamshoro">Jamshoro</a>, it ends in a large delta to the South of <a href="/wiki/Thatta" title="Thatta">Thatta</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> province of Pakistan\n</p><p>The Indus is one of the few rivers in the world to exhibit a <a href="/wiki/Tidal_bore" title="Tidal bore">tidal bore</a>. The Indus system is largely fed by the snows and glaciers of the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a>, Karakoram and the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_Kush" title="Hindu Kush">Hindu Kush</a> ranges of Tibet, the Indian states of <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir" title="Jammu and Kashmir">Jammu and Kashmir</a> and <a href="/wiki/Himachal_Pradesh" title="Himachal Pradesh">Himachal Pradesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gilgit-Baltistan" title="Gilgit-Baltistan">Gilgit-Baltistan</a> region of Pakistan. The flow of the river is also determined by the seasons &#8211; it diminishes greatly in the winter, while flooding its banks in the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> months from July to September. There is also evidence of a steady shift in the course of the river since prehistoric times &#8211; it deviated westwards from flowing into the <a href="/wiki/Rann_of_Kutch" title="Rann of Kutch">Rann of Kutch</a> and adjoining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Banni_grasslands" title="Banni grasslands">Banni grasslands</a> after the 1816 <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquake</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> Presently, Indus water flows in to the <a href="/wiki/Rann_of_Kutch" title="Rann of Kutch">Rann of Kutch</a> during its floods breaching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flood_bank" title="Flood bank">flood banks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kan_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-kan-20">[20]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The traditional source of the river is the <i>Senge Khabab</i> or "Lion\'s Mouth", a perennial spring, not far from the sacred <a href="/wiki/Mount_Kailash" title="Mount Kailash">Mount Kailash</a> marked by a long low line of Tibetan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chortens" title="Chortens">chortens</a>. There are several other tributaries nearby, which may possibly form a longer stream than Senge Khabab, but unlike the Senge Khabab, are all dependent on <a href="/wiki/Snowmelt" title="Snowmelt">snowmelt</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Zanskar_River" title="Zanskar River">Zanskar River</a>, which flows into the Indus in Ladakh, has a greater volume of water than the Indus itself before that point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Albinia_2008,_p._307_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Albinia_2008,_p._307-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:IVC_Map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="459" decoding="async" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/IVC_Map.png/280px-IVC_Map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/IVC_Map.png/420px-IVC_Map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0c/IVC_Map.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:IVC_Map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Extent and major sites of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Indus Valley Civilisation</a> 3000&#160;BC</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilization" title="Indus Valley Civilization">Indus Valley Civilization</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Sindh" title="History of Sindh">History of Sindh</a></div>\n<p>The major cities of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Indus Valley Civilisation</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Harappa" title="Harappa">Harappa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a>, date back to around 3300&#160;BC, and represent some of the largest human habitations of the ancient world. The Indus Valley Civilisation extended from across northeast Afghanistan to Pakistan and northwest India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> with an upward reach from east of <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Jhelum River</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ropar" title="Ropar">Ropar</a> on the upper Sutlej. The coastal settlements extended from <a href="/wiki/Sutkagan_Dor" title="Sutkagan Dor">Sutkagan Dor</a> at the Pakistan, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> border to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kutch" title="Kutch">Kutch</a> in modern <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a>, India. There is an Indus site on the <a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Amu Darya</a> at Shortughai in northern Afghanistan, and the Indus site <a href="/wiki/Alamgirpur" title="Alamgirpur">Alamgirpur</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Hindon_River" title="Hindon River">Hindon River</a> is located only 28&#160;km (17&#160;mi) from <a href="/wiki/Delhi" title="Delhi">Delhi</a>. To date, over 1,052 cities and settlements have been found, mainly in the general region of the <a href="/wiki/Ghaggar-Hakra_River" title="Ghaggar-Hakra River">Ghaggar-Hakra River</a> and its tributaries. Among the settlements were the major urban centres of Harappa and Mohenjo-daro, as well as <a href="/wiki/Lothal" title="Lothal">Lothal</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dholavira" title="Dholavira">Dholavira</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ganeriwala" title="Ganeriwala">Ganeriwala</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rakhigarhi" title="Rakhigarhi">Rakhigarhi</a>. Only 90&#8211;96 of more than 800 known Indus Valley sites have been discovered on the Indus and its tributaries.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sutlej" title="Sutlej">Sutlej</a>, now a tributary of the Indus, in Harappan times flowed into the Ghaggar-Hakra River, in the watershed of which were more Harappan sites than along the Indus.\n</p><p>Most scholars believe that settlements of <a href="/wiki/Gandhara_grave_culture" title="Gandhara grave culture">Gandhara grave culture</a> of the early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryans" title="Indo-Aryans">Indo-Aryans</a> flourished in <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a> from 1700&#160;BC to 600&#160;BC, when <a href="/wiki/Mohenjo-daro" title="Mohenjo-daro">Mohenjo-daro</a> and Harappa had already been abandoned.\n</p><p>The word <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etymology_of_India" title="Etymology of India">"India"</a> is derived from the Indus River. In ancient times, "India" initially referred to those regions immediately along the east bank of the Indus, but by 300&#160;BC, Greek writers including <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Megasthenes" title="Megasthenes">Megasthenes</a> were applying the term to the entire subcontinent that extends much farther eastward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HerodotusIndia_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-HerodotusIndia-24">[24]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The lower basin of the Indus forms a natural boundary between the <a href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>; this region embraces all or parts of the Pakistani provinces <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balochistan_(Pakistan)" title="Balochistan (Pakistan)">Balochistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">Khyber Pakhtunkhwa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> and the countries Afghanistan and India. It was crossed by the invading armies of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander</a>, but after his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonians</a> conquered the west bank&#8212;joining it to the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Hellenic</a> Empire, they elected to retreat along the southern course of the river, ending Alexander\'s Asian campaign. The Indus plains were later dominated by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_empire" title="Persian empire">Persian empire</a> and then the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kushan_empire" title="Kushan empire">Kushan empire</a>. Over several centuries <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslim</a> armies of <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_bin_Qasim" title="Muhammad bin Qasim">Muhammad bin Qasim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mahmud_of_Ghazni" title="Mahmud of Ghazni">Mahmud of Ghazni</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mohammed_Ghori" title="Mohammed Ghori">Mohammed Ghori</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tamerlane" title="Tamerlane">Tamerlane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> crossed the river to invade the inner regions of the <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a> and points farther south and east\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:center; max-width:858px;">\n<div class="thumbinner"><div class="noresize overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus_Valley_near_Leh.jpg" title="The Indus River near Leh, Ladakh"><img alt="" data-file-height="1920" data-file-width="8000" decoding="async" height="204" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Indus_Valley_near_Leh.jpg/850px-Indus_Valley_near_Leh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Indus_Valley_near_Leh.jpg/1275px-Indus_Valley_near_Leh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Indus_Valley_near_Leh.jpg/1700px-Indus_Valley_near_Leh.jpg 2x" width="850"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Indus_Valley_near_Leh.jpg" title="File:Indus Valley near Leh.jpg"> </a></div>The Indus River near Leh, Ladakh</div>\n</div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tributaries">Tributaries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Tributaries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 10em; -webkit-column-width: 10em; column-width: 10em;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Beas River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Chenab_River" title="Chenab River">Chenab River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gar_Tsangpo" title="Gar Tsangpo">Gar River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gilgit_River" title="Gilgit River">Gilgit River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gomal_River" title="Gomal River">Gomal River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Hunza_River" title="Hunza River">Hunza River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Jhelum River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kabul_River" title="Kabul River">Kabul River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kunar_River" title="Kunar River">Kunar River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kurram_River" title="Kurram River">Kurram River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Panjnad_River" title="Panjnad River">Panjnad River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Shyok_River" title="Shyok River">Shyok River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Soan_River" title="Soan River">Soan River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Suru_River_(Indus)" title="Suru River (Indus)">Suru River</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Satluj_River" title="Satluj River">Satluj River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Swat_River" title="Swat River">Swat River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Zanskar_River" title="Zanskar River">Zanskar River</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Zhob_River" title="Zhob River">Zhob River</a></li></ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geology">Geology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Geology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus_river_from_karakouram_highway.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="768" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Indus_river_from_karakouram_highway.jpg/220px-Indus_river_from_karakouram_highway.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Indus_river_from_karakouram_highway.jpg/330px-Indus_river_from_karakouram_highway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Indus_river_from_karakouram_highway.jpg/440px-Indus_river_from_karakouram_highway.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indus_river_from_karakouram_highway.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indus River viewed from the <a href="/wiki/Karakoram_Highway" title="Karakoram Highway">Karakoram Highway</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus_river_near_Leh.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1328" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Indus_river_near_Leh.jpg/280px-Indus_river_near_Leh.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Indus_river_near_Leh.jpg/420px-Indus_river_near_Leh.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Indus_river_near_Leh.jpg/560px-Indus_river_near_Leh.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indus_river_near_Leh.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Indus River near Leh, India, 2014</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg/280px-Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg/420px-Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg/560px-Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Confluence_of_Indus_and_Zanskar_rivers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Confluence of Indus and Zanskar rivers. The Indus is at the left of the picture, flowing left-to-right; the Zanskar, carrying more water, comes in from the top of the picture.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Indus river feeds the Indus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Submarine_fan" title="Submarine fan">submarine fan</a>, which is the second largest sediment body on the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> It consists of around 5&#160;million cubic kilometres of material eroded from the mountains. Studies of the sediment in the modern river indicate that the <a href="/wiki/Karakoram" title="Karakoram">Karakoram Mountains</a> in northern Pakistan and India are the single most important source of material, with the Himalayas providing the next largest contribution, mostly via the large rivers of the Punjab (Jhelum, Ravi, Chenab, Beas and Sutlej). Analysis of sediments from the Arabian Sea has demonstrated that prior to five million years ago the Indus was not connected to these <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a> rivers which instead flowed east into the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganga</a> and were captured after that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> Earlier work showed that sand and silt from western Tibet was reaching the Arabian Sea by 45&#160;million years ago, implying the existence of an ancient Indus River by that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The delta of this proto-Indus river has subsequently been found in the Katawaz Basin, on the Afghan-Pakistan border.\n</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Nanga_Parbat" title="Nanga Parbat">Nanga Parbat</a> region, the massive amounts of erosion due to the Indus river following the capture and rerouting through that area is thought to bring middle and lower crustal rocks to the surface.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In November 2011, satellite images showed that the Indus river had re-entered <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, feeding <a href="/wiki/Great_Rann_of_Kutch" title="Great Rann of Kutch">Great Rann of Kutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Little_Rann_of_Kutch" title="Little Rann of Kutch">Little Rann of Kutch</a> and a lake near <a href="/wiki/Ahmedabad" title="Ahmedabad">Ahmedabad</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Nal_Sarovar_Bird_Sanctuary" title="Nal Sarovar Bird Sanctuary">Nal Sarovar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kan_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-kan-20">[20]</a></sup> Heavy rains had left the river basin along with the <a href="/wiki/Lake_Manchar" title="Lake Manchar">Lake Manchar</a>, Lake Hemal and <a href="/wiki/Keenjhar_Lake" title="Keenjhar Lake">Kalri Lake</a> (all in modern-day Pakistan) inundated. This happened two centuries after the Indus river shifted its course westwards following the <a href="/wiki/1819_Rann_of_Kutch_earthquake" title="1819 Rann of Kutch earthquake">1819 Rann of Kutch earthquake</a>.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Induan" title="Induan">Induan</a> Age at start of the <a href="/wiki/Triassic" title="Triassic">Triassic</a> Period of geological time is named for the Indus region.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Wildlife">Wildlife</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Wildlife">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus_river,_Pakistan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1571" data-file-width="2299" decoding="async" height="191" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Indus_river%2C_Pakistan.jpg/280px-Indus_river%2C_Pakistan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Indus_river%2C_Pakistan.jpg/420px-Indus_river%2C_Pakistan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Indus_river%2C_Pakistan.jpg/560px-Indus_river%2C_Pakistan.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indus_river,_Pakistan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Footbridge on the Indus River in Pakistan</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fishermen_on_the_Indus.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="346" data-file-width="598" decoding="async" height="162" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Fishermen_on_the_Indus.jpeg/280px-Fishermen_on_the_Indus.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Fishermen_on_the_Indus.jpeg/420px-Fishermen_on_the_Indus.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Fishermen_on_the_Indus.jpeg/560px-Fishermen_on_the_Indus.jpeg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fishermen_on_the_Indus.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Fishermen on the Indus River, c. 1905</div></div></div>\n<p>Accounts of the Indus valley from the times of Alexander\'s campaign indicate a healthy forest cover in the region, which has now considerably receded. The Mughal Emperor <a href="/wiki/Babur" title="Babur">Babur</a> writes of encountering rhinoceroses along its bank in his memoirs (the <a href="/wiki/Baburnama" title="Baburnama">Baburnama</a>). Extensive <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> and human interference in the ecology of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shivalik_Hills" title="Shivalik Hills">Shivalik Hills</a> has led to a marked deterioration in vegetation and growing conditions. The Indus valley regions are arid with poor vegetation. Agriculture is sustained largely due to irrigation works.\nThe Indus river and its watershed has a rich biodiversity. It is home to around 25 amphibian species and 147 fish species, 22 of which are only found in the Indus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mammals">Mammals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mammals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_River_Dolphin" title="Indus River Dolphin">Indus River Dolphin</a> (<i>Platanista indicus minor</i>) is a sub-species of dolphin found only in the Indus River. It formerly also occurred in the tributaries of the Indus river. According to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World_Wildlife_Fund" title="World Wildlife Fund">World Wildlife Fund</a> it is one of the most threatened <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cetaceans" title="Cetaceans">cetaceans</a> with only about 1,000 still existing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fish">Fish</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Fish">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Palla fish, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenualosa_ilisha" title="Tenualosa ilisha">Tenualosa ilisha</a>, of the river is a delicacy for people living along the river. The population of fish in the river is moderately high, with <a href="/wiki/Sukkur" title="Sukkur">Sukkur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thatta" title="Thatta">Thatta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kotri" title="Kotri">Kotri</a> being the major fishing centres &#8211; all in the lower Sindh course. As a result, damming and irrigation has made fish farming an important economic activity. Located southeast of Karachi, the large delta has been recognized by conservationists as one of the world\'s most important ecological regions. Here, the river turns into many marshes, streams and creeks and meets the sea at shallow levels. Here, marine fishes are found in abundance, including <a href="/wiki/Pomfret" title="Pomfret">pomfret</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prawns" title="Prawns">prawns</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The Indus is the most important supplier of water resources to the <a href="/wiki/Punjab" title="Punjab">Punjab</a> and Sindh plains &#8211; it forms the backbone of agriculture and food production in Pakistan. The river is especially critical since rainfall is meagre in the lower Indus valley. Irrigation canals were first built by the people of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_Civilisation" title="Indus Valley Civilisation">Indus Valley Civilisation</a>, and later by the engineers of the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a>. Modern irrigation was introduced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/British_East_India_Company" title="British East India Company">British East India Company</a> in 1850 &#8211; the construction of modern canals accompanied with the restoration of old canals. The British supervised the construction of one of the most complex irrigation networks in the world. The <a href="/wiki/Guddu_Barrage" title="Guddu Barrage">Guddu Barrage</a> is 1,350&#160;m (4,430&#160;ft) long &#8211; irrigating <a href="/wiki/Sukkur" title="Sukkur">Sukkur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jacobabad" title="Jacobabad">Jacobabad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Larkana" title="Larkana">Larkana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kalat_District" title="Kalat District">Kalat</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Sukkur_Barrage" title="Sukkur Barrage">Sukkur Barrage</a> serves over 20,000&#160;km<sup>2</sup> (7,700&#160;sq&#160;mi).\n</p><p>After Pakistan came into existence, a <a href="/wiki/Indus_Waters_Treaty" title="Indus Waters Treaty">water control treaty signed between India and Pakistan in 1960</a> guaranteed that Pakistan would receive water from the Indus River and its two tributaries the <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Jhelum River</a> &amp; the <a href="/wiki/Chenab_River" title="Chenab River">Chenab River</a> independently of upstream control by India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Indus_Basin_Project" title="Indus Basin Project">Indus Basin Project</a> consisted primarily of the construction of two main dams, the <a href="/wiki/Mangla_Dam" title="Mangla Dam">Mangla Dam</a> built on the Jhelum River and the <a href="/wiki/Tarbela_Dam" title="Tarbela Dam">Tarbela Dam</a> constructed on the Indus River, together with their subsidiary dams.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The Pakistan Water and Power Development Authority undertook the construction of the Chashma-Jhelum link canal &#8211; linking the waters of the Indus and Jhelum rivers &#8211; extending water supplies to the regions of <a href="/wiki/Bahawalpur" title="Bahawalpur">Bahawalpur</a> and <a href="/wiki/Multan" title="Multan">Multan</a>. Pakistan constructed the <a href="/wiki/Tarbela_Dam" title="Tarbela Dam">Tarbela Dam</a> near <a href="/wiki/Rawalpindi" title="Rawalpindi">Rawalpindi</a> &#8211; standing 2,743 metres (9,000&#160;ft) long and 143 metres (470&#160;ft) high, with an 80-kilometre (50&#160;mi) long reservoir. It supports the <a href="/wiki/Chashma_Barrage" title="Chashma Barrage">Chashma Barrage</a> near Dera Ismail Khan for irrigation use and flood control and the <a href="/wiki/Taunsa_Barrage" title="Taunsa Barrage">Taunsa Barrage</a> near <a href="/wiki/Dera_Ghazi_Khan" title="Dera Ghazi Khan">Dera Ghazi Khan</a> which also produces 100,000&#160;kilowatts of electricity. The <a href="/wiki/Kotri_Barrage" title="Kotri Barrage">Kotri Barrage</a> near <a href="/wiki/Hyderabad,_Sindh" title="Hyderabad, Sindh">Hyderabad</a> is 915 metres (3,000&#160;ft) long and provides additional water supplies for Karachi. The extensive linking of tributaries with the Indus has helped spread water resources to the valley of <a href="/wiki/Peshawar" title="Peshawar">Peshawar</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">Khyber Pakhtunkhwa</a>. The extensive irrigation and dam projects provide the basis for Pakistan\'s large production of crops such as <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">sugarcane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>. The dams also generate electricity for heavy industries and urban centers.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="People">People</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: People">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus_near_Skardu.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="823" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Indus_near_Skardu.jpg/280px-Indus_near_Skardu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Indus_near_Skardu.jpg/420px-Indus_near_Skardu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Indus_near_Skardu.jpg/560px-Indus_near_Skardu.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indus_near_Skardu.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Indus River near <a href="/wiki/Skardu" title="Skardu">Skardu</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilgit%E2%80%93Baltistan" title="Gilgit&#8211;Baltistan">Gilgit&#8211;Baltistan</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:River_Dubair_tributary_to_Indus_river.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b0/River_Dubair_tributary_to_Indus_river.jpeg/280px-River_Dubair_tributary_to_Indus_river.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b0/River_Dubair_tributary_to_Indus_river.jpeg/420px-River_Dubair_tributary_to_Indus_river.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b0/River_Dubair_tributary_to_Indus_river.jpeg/560px-River_Dubair_tributary_to_Indus_river.jpeg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:River_Dubair_tributary_to_Indus_river.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Dubair Khwarr, a tributary of the Indus, near <a href="/wiki/Shaikhdara" title="Shaikhdara">Shaikhdara</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Khyber_Pakhtunkhwa" title="Khyber Pakhtunkhwa">Khyber Pakhtunkhwa</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The inhabitants of the regions are mainly Muslim as Pakistan is an Islamic country through which the Indus river passes and forms a major natural feature and resource are diverse in ethnicity, religion, national and linguistic backgrounds. On the northern course of the river in the state of <a href="/wiki/Jammu_and_Kashmir" title="Jammu and Kashmir">Jammu and Kashmir</a> in India, live the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> people of <a href="/wiki/Ladakh" title="Ladakh">Ladakh</a>, of <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_people" title="Tibetan people">Tibetan</a> stock, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dards" title="Dards">Dards</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryans" title="Indo-Aryans">Indo-Aryan</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dard_people" title="Dard people">Dardic</a> stock and practising <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. Then it descends into Baltistan, northern Pakistan passing the main Balti city of <a href="/wiki/Skardu" title="Skardu">Skardu</a>. A river from Dubair Bala also drains into it at Dubair Bazar. People living in this area are mainly Kohistani and speak the Kohistani language. Major areas through which the Indus river passes in <a href="/wiki/Kohistan_District,_Pakistan" title="Kohistan District, Pakistan">Kohistan</a> are <a href="/wiki/Dasu" title="Dasu">Dasu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pattan" title="Pattan">Pattan</a> and Dubair. As it continues through Pakistan, the Indus river forms a distinctive boundary of ethnicity and cultures &#8211; upon the western banks the population is largely <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashtun_people" title="Pashtun people">Pashtun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Baloch_people" title="Baloch people">Baloch</a>, and of other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_people" title="Iranian people">Iranian</a> stock. The eastern banks are largely populated by people of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Aryan_people" title="Indo-Aryan people">Indo-Aryan</a> stock, such as the <a href="/wiki/Punjabis" title="Punjabis">Punjabis</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sindhis" title="Sindhis">Sindhis</a>. In northern Punjab and the Khyber Pakhtunkhwa, ethnic Pashtun tribes live alongside <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dard_people" title="Dard people">Dardic</a> people in the hills (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khowar_people" title="Khowar people">Khowar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kalash_people" title="Kalash people">Kalash</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shina_people" title="Shina people">Shina</a>, etc.), Burushos (in <a href="/wiki/Hunza_Valley" title="Hunza Valley">Hunza</a>), and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjabi_people" title="Punjabi people">Punjabi people</a>.\n</p><p>The people living along the Indus river speak Punjabi and Sindhi on the eastern side (in Punjab and Sindh provinces respectively), Pushto plus Balochi as well as Barohi (in Khyber Pakhtoonkha and Baluchistan provinces). In the province of Sindh, the upper third of the river is inhabited by people speaking Saraiki; which is a somewhat transitional dialect of the Punjabi and Sindhi languages.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_issues">Modern issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Modern issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">September 2010</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pakistan_Indus_flooding_July_2010_-_MODIS.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="389" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Pakistan_Indus_flooding_July_2010_-_MODIS.png/280px-Pakistan_Indus_flooding_July_2010_-_MODIS.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Pakistan_Indus_flooding_July_2010_-_MODIS.png/420px-Pakistan_Indus_flooding_July_2010_-_MODIS.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Pakistan_Indus_flooding_July_2010_-_MODIS.png/560px-Pakistan_Indus_flooding_July_2010_-_MODIS.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pakistan_Indus_flooding_July_2010_-_MODIS.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Satellite images of the upper Indus River valley, comparing water-levels on 1 August 2009 (top) and 31 July 2010 (bottom)</div></div></div>\n<p>The Indus is a strategically vital resource for Pakistan\'s economy and society. After <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> and India declared Independence from the <a href="/wiki/British_Raj" title="British Raj">British Raj</a>, the use of the waters of the Indus and its five eastern tributaries became a major dispute between India and Pakistan. The irrigation canals of the <a href="/wiki/Sutlej" title="Sutlej">Sutlej</a> valley and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bari_Doab" title="Bari Doab">Bari Doab</a> were split &#8211; with the canals lying primarily in Pakistan and the headwork dams in India disrupting supply in some parts of Pakistan. The concern over India building large dams over various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Punjab_region" title="Punjab region">Punjab</a> rivers that could undercut the supply flowing to Pakistan, as well as the possibility that India could divert rivers in the time of war, caused political consternation in Pakistan. Holding diplomatic talks brokered by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a>, India and Pakistan signed the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Waters_Treaty" title="Indus Waters Treaty">Indus Waters Treaty</a> in 1960. The treaty gave India control of the three easternmost rivers of the Punjab, the <a href="/wiki/Sutlej" title="Sutlej">Sutlej</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Beas_River" title="Beas River">Beas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi</a>, while Pakistan gained control of the three western rivers, the <a href="/wiki/Jhelum_River" title="Jhelum River">Jhelum</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chenab_River" title="Chenab River">Chenab</a> and the Indus. India retained the right to use of the western rivers for non-irrigation projects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Large-scale diversion of the river\'s water for irrigation has raised far-reaching issues. Sediment clogging from poor maintenance of canals has affected agricultural production and vegetation on numerous occasions. Irrigation itself is increasing soil salinization, reducing crop yields and in some cases rendering farmland useless for cultivation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> And ecologically, the reduced flow of fresh water and silt into the Indus delta is threatening the area\'s mangrove forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mangrove-degradation_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-mangrove-degradation-35">[35]</a></sup>\n</p><p>There are also concerns that the Indus River may be shifting its course westwards, although the progression spans centuries.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Indus_delta">Indus delta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Indus delta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indus_River_Delta" title="Indus River Delta">Indus River Delta</a></div>\n<p>Originally, the delta used to receive almost all of the water from the Indus river, which has an annual flow of approximately 180&#160;billion cubic metres (240&#160;billion cubic yards), and is accompanied by 400&#160;million tonnes (390&#160;million long tons) of silt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Since the 1940s, dams, barrages and irrigation works have been constructed on the river Indus. The Indus Basin Irrigation System is the "largest contiguous irrigation system developed over the past 140 years" anywhere in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> This has reduced the flow of water and by 2018, the average annual flow of water below the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kotri_barrage" title="Kotri barrage">Kotri barrage</a> was 33&#160;billion cubic metres (43&#160;billion cubic yards),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> and annual amount of silt discharged was estimated at 100&#160;million tonnes (98&#160;million long tons).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mangrove-degradation_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-mangrove-degradation-35">[35]</a></sup> As a result, the <a href="/wiki/2010_Pakistan_floods" title="2010 Pakistan floods">2010 Pakistan floods</a> were considered "good news" for the ecosystem and population of the river delta as they brought much needed fresh water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian1_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian1-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guardian2_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-guardian2-40">[40]</a></sup> Any further utilization of the river basin water is not economically feasible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Vegetation and wildlife of the Indus delta are threatened by the reduced inflow of fresh water, along with extensive deforestation, industrial pollution and <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. Damming has also isolated the delta population of Indus River dolphins from those further upstream.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_of_climate_change_on_the_river">Effects of climate change on the river</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Effects of climate change on the river">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Plateau" title="Tibetan Plateau">Tibetan Plateau</a> contains the world\'s third-largest store of ice. Qin Dahe, the former head of the China Meteorological Administration, said the recent<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (January 2019)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> fast pace of melting and warmer temperatures will be good for agriculture and tourism in the short term, but issued a strong warning:\n</p>\n<dl><dd><dl><dd>"Temperatures are rising four times faster than elsewhere in China, and the Tibetan glaciers are retreating at a higher speed than in any other part of the world... In the short term, this will cause lakes to expand and bring floods and mudflows.. In the long run, the glaciers are vital lifelines of the Indus River. Once they vanish, water supplies in Pakistan will be in peril."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></dd></dl></dd></dl>\n<p>"There is insufficient data to say what will happen to the Indus," says David Grey, the World Bank\'s senior water advisor in South Asia. "But we all have very nasty fears that the flows of the Indus could be severely, severely affected by glacier melt as a consequence of climate change," and reduced by perhaps as much as 50 percent. "Now what does that mean to a population that lives in a desert [where], without the river, there would be no life? I don\'t know the answer to that question," he says. "But we need to be concerned about that. Deeply, deeply concerned."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p><p>U.S. diplomat Richard Holbrooke said, shortly before his death in 2010, that he believed that falling water levels in the Indus River "could very well precipitate World War III."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution">Pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>Over the years factories on the banks of the Indus River have increased levels of water pollution in the river and the atmosphere around it. High levels of pollutants in the river have led to the deaths of endangered Indus River Dolphin. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sindh_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="Sindh Environmental Protection Agency">Sindh Environmental Protection Agency</a> has ordered polluting factories around the river to shut down under the Pakistan Environmental Protection Act, 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Polluting_factory_shut_down_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Polluting_factory_shut_down-47">[47]</a></sup> Death of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indus_River_Dolphin" title="Indus River Dolphin">Indus River Dolphin</a> has also been attributed to fishermen using poison to kill fish and scooping them up.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fishing_poison_1_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fishing_poison_1-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fishing_poison_2_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fishing_poison_2-49">[49]</a></sup> As a result, the government banned fishing from <a href="/wiki/Guddu_Barrage" title="Guddu Barrage">Guddu Barrage</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sukkur" title="Sukkur">Sukkur</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Indus_Fishing_Ban_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Indus_Fishing_Ban-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Coming second after the <a href="/wiki/Yangtze" title="Yangtze">Yangtze</a>, and together with 9 other rivers, the Indus transports 90&#160;% of all the <a href="/wiki/Plastic_pollution" title="Plastic pollution">plastic</a> that reaches the oceans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2010_floods">2010 floods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: 2010 floods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus_flooding_2010_en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="726" data-file-width="856" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Indus_flooding_2010_en.svg/280px-Indus_flooding_2010_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Indus_flooding_2010_en.svg/420px-Indus_flooding_2010_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Indus_flooding_2010_en.svg/560px-Indus_flooding_2010_en.svg.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indus_flooding_2010_en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Affected areas as of 26 August 2010</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2010_Pakistan_floods" title="2010 Pakistan floods">2010 Pakistan floods</a></div>\n<p>Frequently, Indus river is prone to moderate to severe flooding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> In July 2010, following abnormally heavy <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> rains, the Indus River rose above its banks and started flooding. The rain continued for the next two months, devastating large areas of Pakistan. In <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>, the Indus burst its banks near <a href="/wiki/Sukkur" title="Sukkur">Sukkur</a> on 8 August, submerging the village of Mor Khan Jatoi.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodeen_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodeen-54">[54]</a></sup> In early August, the heaviest flooding moved southward along the Indus River from severely affected northern regions toward western <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">Punjab</a>, where at least 1,400,000 acres (570,000&#160;ha) of cropland was destroyed, and the southern province of Sindh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BBC7_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-BBC7-55">[55]</a></sup> As of  September 2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, over two thousand people had died and over a million homes had been destroyed since the flooding began.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2011_floods">2011 floods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: 2011 floods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/2011_Sindh_floods" title="2011 Sindh floods">2011 Sindh floods</a></div>\n<p>The 2011 <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a> floods began during the Pakistani monsoon season in mid-August 2011, resulting from heavy monsoon rains in Sindh, eastern Balochistan, and southern Punjab.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-58">[58]</a></sup> The floods caused considerable damage; an estimated 434 civilians were killed, with 5.3&#160;million people and 1,524,773 homes affected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-et_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-et-59">[59]</a></sup> Sindh is a fertile region and often called the "breadbasket" of the country; the damage and toll of the floods on the local agrarian economy was said to be extensive. At least 1.7&#160;million <a href="/wiki/Acre" title="Acre">acres</a> (690,000&#160;<a href="/wiki/Hectare" title="Hectare">ha</a>; 2,700&#160;<a href="/wiki/Square_mile" title="Square mile">sq&#160;mi</a>) of arable land were inundated. The flooding followed the previous year\'s floods, which devastated a large part of the country.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-et_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-et-59">[59]</a></sup> Unprecedented torrential monsoon rains caused severe flooding in 16 districts of Sindh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pakmet1_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-pakmet1-60">[60]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Barrages.2C_bridges_and_dams"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Barrages,_bridges_and_dams">Barrages, bridges and dams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Indus_River&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Barrages, bridges and dams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>In Pakistan currently there are three barrages on the Indus: Guddu barrage, Sukkur Barrage, and Kotri barrage (also called Ghulam Muhammad barrage). There are some bridges on river Indus, such as, Dadu Moro Bridge, Larkana Khairpur Indus River Bridge, Thatta-Sujawal bridge, Jhirk-Mula Katiar bridge and recently planned Kandhkot-Ghotki bridge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Kala Bagh Barrage, <a href="/wiki/Chashma_Barrage" title="Chashma Barrage">Chasma Barrage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taunsa_Barrage" title="Taunsa Barrage">Taunsa Barrage</a> are also built in <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">Punjab</a> on the Indus.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Tarbela_Dam" title="Tarbela Dam">Tarbela Dam</a> in Pakistan is constructed on the Indus River, while the controversial <a href="/wiki/Kalabagh_Dam" title="Kalabagh Dam">Kalabagh dam</a> is also being constructed on Indus river.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gallery">Gallery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a
